 PART A Introduction 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 i. Preface The present edition of the Regula Saluatoris constitutes the first volume in the series of the Opera minora of St. Birgitta of Sweden and is followed by the previously published Sermo Angelicus. The textual history of the Regula Saluatoris is almost completely different from that of the other Birgittine texts, which rendered it impossible to rely on previous research into the Birgitta corpus and made it necessary to devote time to collecting and classifying manu- scripts. The entire work, including the printing, has been carried out under the auspices of Kungl. Vitterhets Historie och Antikvitets Akademien (Royal Academy of Letters, History and Antiquities) in Stockholm. Uppsala, December 1975 Sten Eklund 
 
 
 
 
 2. Lists of manuscripts, printed literature and abbreviations 2.1. Latin manuscripts As regards the dates of the MSS, I do not go into details in the lists and more information and discussions are often provided in the literature cited for each MS. Concerning the contents of the MSS, I describe the RS text only, giving the paragraphs of the RS text which each MS has and indicating which of the six RS versions (i.e. the II, E, (I), A, IP, 0 versions, see sections 3-3.6) the text belongs to and the position it holds in the MS. Small Greek index letters are used to distinguish different parts of one and the same MS whenever these parts have different ancestors and, accordingly, different positions in the stemma, for example, Q. and Q0 (§§ 1-44 of the RS text in Q [= Qa] are copied from a II MS, §§ 47-141 [= Q13 ] from a E MS) or Ce., CO, Ge, CeS, Cev (the e MS contains the II text [= Va], the E text written twice [= Co and Cel, some small parts of the II text written separately [= and Cl and traces of the '1' text [= C"; see note 3]). For the sake of con- venience, the abbreviation MS(S) is used of such parts of one and the same MS, so that, for example, Cea is called a MS, although it is, strictly speaking, only part of the Ce MS. It should be mentioned also that, whenever I speak of the H MSS, I include the editio princeps, designated Gh, although it is not in reality a MS. For very valuable information on some of the Latin MSS, I am obliged to Dr. Tore Nyberg, of Odense. F = Lund, Univ. Lib., Cod. 21 (»Codex Falkenberg»), about 1400? Lit.: Bergh, Rev. VII, pp. 6o f. and Bergh, Rev. V, p. 7, with further references; Eklund, SA, p. io. Folio 341": §§ 296-302 of the H or the (D text. Folio 341 y: §§ 285-286 of the H or the D text. K = Kalmar, High School Lib. (»Läroverksbiblioteket»), about 1400. Lit.: Bergh, Rev. VII, pp. 61 f. and Bergh, Rev. V, p. 7, with further references; Eklund, SA, p. 10. Folios 220r-225v: §§ 1-317 of the H text. L = London, British Museum, God. Harleianus 612, i5th century (middle part). Lit.: Bergh, Rev. VII, p. 42, with further references; Eklund, SA, p. 13, with further references. Columns 636-642: §§ 1-317 of the H text. 0 = Oxford, Balliol College, MS 225, i5th century (first half). Lit.: Bergh, Rev. VII, p. 42, with further references; Bergh, Rev. V, p. 7, with further references; Eklund, SA, p. 10. Oa = folios 216v-222r: §§ 45a-27o of the E text.' There are, however, other parts in which the E text on folios 24r-51r contains corrections of words in which the 'P text differs from the E text and they provoke the suspicion that there was 'P text from the beginning in these passages as weil (especially in § 84 [fol. 271 and §§ 249-25o [foll. 48v-491; in § 250 the words solus qui of the I' text are still legible under the correction sola que [E text]). It seems as if folios 24r-51r from the beginning contained E text with features from the 'P text intermingled in it (in §§ 185-191, however, the pure 'P text), which features were afterwards removed by corrections. The designation Ce6 has been used for the original I' text in §§ 185-191 (foll. 39v-41r) and the designation Ce0 for the E text surrounding §§ 185-191 as well as for the corrected text (E text) in these para- graphs.—Apart from the features of the 'P text, the Ce0 MS is also contaminated from the H text (subsection 4.3.8) and, accordingly, provides a veritable mixture.

I give here a list of the Old Swedish MSS preceded by some in- formation on them in smaller type. 2.2. Concerning the OSA MS it should be observed that Klemming printed the OSAa and the OSAR texts one after the other but that they have different positions in the MS. It cannot be taken for granted that OSAR has the same location in the stemma as OSAa and, accordingly, OSAR must not be used in the same way as OSAa in establishing the II text (cf. note 133). The OSAR text may be either a SI or a II text (or maybe even a eto or a A text) and the decision is a hard one, since the OSAR text is short (my cursory inquiry did not yield any reliable results; the OSAR text, however, seems to have formed a unit together with the Ex., since the OSAR chapters are numbered together with the Ex.).—The text in OSB appears to have similarities with both the II, the E and the texts (cf. Gussgard, pp. 61 f. and 76 f. and Montag, pp. 142 f.). It would take an ex- tensive investigation to determine its position, but it seems to yield nothing of use in the establishment of the Latin texts.—The OSC and OSD MSS contain fragments of one and the same Old Swedish text version, as is clear from the paragraphs which they have in common. This text is an S/ (or a II) text but a different version than the text in OSAa. Both Ståhle (pp. 8 f.) and Gussgard (pp. 74 f.) think that the OSC/OSD text is very old, probably older than the OSAa text, although there is no absolutely convincing evidence.—The OSE MS, finally, contains the regulations enacted by Nils König, Bishop of Linköping, in 1452; these regulations are very dissimilar to the authentic RS texts and of no value in reconstructing them. 11 In o, §§ 185-191 of the 1' '7 text (= of1) are inserted between §§ 267 and 268 of the E text (= oc); cf. 0. 12 I would like to stress the fact that I examined the Old Swedish texts only in order to get some help in establishing the Latin texts. The Old Swedish versions, their internal relations and their relations to the Latin texts offer several problems and it would probably be possible to achieve more reliable results in this respect now that the Latin texts have been edited.

When a part of one of the texts has no counterpart in one or both of the other texts, the paragraph number(s) are missing in these (for example, §§ 35-41 are in the (Dtext but not in the H and E texts, §§ 42-44 are in the II and 01:0 texts but not in the E text and §§ 45a-47 are in the E text but not in the H and 1 texts, whereas §§ 50-52 are in all three texts; in other words, a gap in the series of paragraphs in one text indicates that one or both of the other texts have something excessive).  3. The history of the Regula Saluatoris There exist several versions of the RS. Already in medieval documents (even in some of the RS MSS), there is a distinction between regula in prima persona (which means that Christ appears speaking in the first person, as is the case in the H, D and A texts) and regula in tercia persona (Christ is referred to in the third person, as is the case in the E and 'P texts). These designations sometimes appear in works of modern scholars as weil, but they are completely insufficient. Montag (pp. 124-15o, especially p. 144) improved the situation by introducing the designations "die Friihfassung" (St. Birgitta's own text = SL fl), "die ap- probierte Fassung" (the bull of 1378 = E) and "die adaptierte Fassung" (that mixture of Birgitta's own text and the bull of 1378 which has been termed (1) below). In the present edition it proved necessary to distinguish seven text versions of the RS and for this purpose seven Greek capital letters have been used (which also symbolize the archetypes of the seven texts in the stemma). In sections 3.1-3.6 below, these texts are dealt with in chronological order, viz. SZ II0E 413 A 'P." 3.1. The S2 and II texts St. Birgitta relates herself that she conceived the RS in a very short time (RS II 280-285) and that it was rapidly written down a few days afterwards by a friend of God's (RS H 286), i.e. by her confessor, Prior Petrus (Ex. 45; Höjer, pp• 33 f.; Westman, pp. 15 f.). This happened in Vadstena some time between 1342 and 1349 (see below). Now, the H text is the oldest text of Birgitta's own version that can be en- tirely reconstructed from the extant MSS. The SI text is a still older stage (see the stemma picture on p. 3o), but the SI text cannot be completely re- constructed and for that reason the H text has been edited here instead of the SI text (section 5.4 It is obvious that the II text consists of a core (conceived by Birgitta in Vadstena, as described above) and later additions. RS II 290-303, for example, were written several years after the actual core, as is clear from the words deinde plures preterierunt anni in RS II 290. The extent of the original core of the RS may be subject to discussion, but perhaps Wessn is right in assuming that it comprised RS H 50-267 (except the headings) and that RS n i, 5-34, 44, 273-317 and all the headings were added gradually afterwards (Wess&i, pp. 47-67; cf. also Montag, p. 1251). As regards the additions to the original core, the place of origin and the date are dubious. In the edition four of these texts have been printed, viz. II E (1) 0; although 0 is older than E and 1, it is nevertheless placed at the end, since H E (1) are more similar to each other and 15 have the same paragraph numbering, whereas O requires separate paragraph numbers, owing to its specific shape (subsection 2.4.1). 
 
 
 
 
 22 3. THE HISTORY OF THE REGULA SALVATORIS The statement that the original core of the RS was conceived in Vadstena some time between 1342 and 1349 requires some comments. It is said explicitly in incipits in the II text that St. Birgitta received the RS in Sweden (RS H 12 in eodem regno Swecie), more precisely, in Vadstena (RS H i in castro Watzsteni Lincopensis dyocesis in regno Swecie).16 Yet the words in dominio regis norwegie in RS H 5 have been taken to indicate that the RS was revealed to St. Birgitta in Norway." We know that she undertook a pilgrimage to Norway (A&P, pp. 14, 309 and 579)18 and some scholars have assumed that the first inspiration appeared during this pilgrimage and that the RS was then worked out in detail on Birgitta's return to Vadstena (Höjer, p. 33; Steffen, pp. X f.; H. Schiick, ISLH i, p. 185; Lindblom, pp. 25 ff.). Others have rejected the idea of Norway as the place of origin, assuming that RS H 5—II, which do not belong to the original core of the RS, were written in Rome and that the mention of Norway is merely due to ig- norance of the geography of the northern countries (Flavigny, p. 1501; Westman, p. 9 25; Wessål, pp. 63 f.).' An analysis, however, of RS H 5-11 reveals that the words in dominio regis Norwegie do not necessarily mean that the RS was conceived by St. Birgitta in Norway. While she was staying in dominio regis Norwegie, her soul began (cepit) to be perfectly vigorous in seeing, hearing, talking and feeling spiritual things (§§ 5-6), so that she often heard many things being told to her spiritually (§ 7). Afterwards she showed them to the Archbishop of Uppsala and some other persons, who examined them and declared that they were genuine and not a delusion of the Devil (§§ 8-9). In such a revelation (in tali eciam reuelacione) she once (semel) saw two persons and heard a voice declaring that they were Christ and his mother Mary; this having been said, Christ opened his mouth and dictated the RS (§§ Io—I i). There is no basis in this for assuming that the RS was revealed in dominio regis Norwegie; it is only said that St. Birgitta started having revela- tions in dominio regis Norwegie2° and that Christ once dictated the RS to her in a revela- tion of this kind (in tali eciam reuelacione semel). 16 The entire incipit, i.e. § i, to which these words belong, appears also in the Old Swedish version (OSAa, see Klemming 4, p. 3). Wessn (p. 67) has noticed the words there (wppa watzstena sloot j lynköpungx biscopsdöme j swerikis rike) but he erroneously says that they have no counter- part whatsoever in the Latin text. The reason for this mistake is probably that a shorter version of the incipit, which does not mention Vadstena or Linköping, happened to be printed in the editio princeps, Gh (for the two incipits and their distribution, see subsections 4.2.1 and 5.1.1 (e)). 17 People were bewildered by the words in dominio regis norwegie as early as the i5th century, as can be seen from the following explanation in the pY MS (it forms a heading preceding § 5): Hec reuelacio, vt creditur, facta fuit domine Birgitte circa fines Lodosie, que est sita in regno Noruegie, vbi eciam plures reuelaciones in volumine magno contentas dicitur habuisse. 18 It is open to doubt when this pilgrimage took place: the words dum viuebat suprascriptus maritus suus in A&P, pp. 14 and 3og, are of no value in this respect ,as they do not necessarily refer to the stay in Norway. In fact H. Schiick (ISLH i, p. 185) declares that it was shortly after her husband's death, whereas Steffen (p. VIII) admits that it is open to doubt but that it was probably before his death. 19 The idea that norwegie is an error for nericie is discussed by Wessn (pp. 64 f.), who finds it not very likely. 20 If the words in dominio regis norwegie refer to the stay in Norway, this fits weil chronologi- cally. St. Birgitta was probably in Norway shortly before or shortly after her husband's death (note 18) and she began to have revelations in large numbers shortly after her husband's death (Höjer, p. 32; Westman, pp. 99-116). 
 
 
 
 
 3.1. THE n AND H TEXTS 23 So much for the place of origin. There is no explicit information on the date of origin, so one is reduced to drawing conclusions. It is obvious from the account above that the original core of the RS was conceived before St. Birgitta left Sweden for Rome (from where she did not return until her death). It seems quite clear that Birgitta was in Rome during the Holy Year 135o (A&P, pp. 12 [articulus V], 353, 415 and 449); accordingly I have regarded 1349 as the last possible year for the conception.21 There is no obvious terminus post quem; it is generally held that it was not until the return from Santiago de Compostella in 1342 that St. Birgitta and her husband had precise plans of founding a monastery and not until after her husband's death a few years later that St. Birgitta had revelations in large numbers (note 20). Thus it seems unlikely that the core of the RS should have been conceived earlier than 1342 and maybe it was not until after her husband's death in 1 34422 (Cnattingius [p. 17] frankly states that Birgitta's husband was dead when she conceived the RS; Lindblom [p. 29] seems to hold the same opinion). In the literature, however, one often comes across specific dates somewhere around 1345 (Flavigny, p. 182 [before 1346]; Montag, p. 124 [about 1345]; Sunckn, p. 136 [probably 1345]; Lindblom, pp. 24 and 29 [in good time before the ist of May 1346]). Only some of the scholars give arguments for being specific and, on the whole, the date seems to rest on the assumption that 1344 constitutes a terminus post quem with regard to her husband's death (above) and 1346 a terminus ante quem with regard to two other events. On the ist of May 1346, King Magnus and Queen Blanka bequeathed the Royal estate at Vadstena to be converted into a monastery by St. Birgitta (DS 4069 [cf. also DS 420o and 6574]). Accordingly, the RS should have existed by then (thus Steffen, pp. XIV and 306; Wess&i, p. 52; Surickn, p. 16; Lindblom, pp. 24 and 29). This argumenta- tion, however, although not completely without value, only proves that St. Birgitta had advanced plans of founding a monastery in 1346 but not necessarily that she thought of founding a new order and had conceived the RS (cf. Klockars, Biskop Hemming, p. 170). The second reason is still more feeble. Some time in the second half of the 1340s, Bishop Hemming, of Åbo, and St. Birgitta's confessor, Prior Petrus, brought messages from St. Birgitta to Clement VI and to the Kings of France and England. Some scholars have assumed that Bishop Hemming on this occasion also presented the RS to the Pope (Steffen, pp. XIV and 306; Wess&i, pp. 51 f.; Sund&i, pp. 15 f.; Lindblom, p. 3o; cf. also Kraft, pp. 185 ff.). Apart from the fact that the date of the journey has been open to doubt and the journey apparently took place at a later date than 1346,23 there is no evidence whatsoever in the medieval sources that Bishop Hemming brought the RS with him and the idea is mere speculation (cf. Y. Brilioth, Svenska kyrkans historia II, Den senare medeltiden 1274-1521, Stockholm 1941, p. 199 and 205; 1. Andersson, Källstudier till Sveriges historia 1230-1436, Lund 1928, p. 1361; Klockars, Biskop Hemming, pp. 168-172). Accordingly, it is safer not to adopt 1346 as a terminus ante quem. 21 It has been discussed whether Birgitta left Sweden in 1349 or as early as 1346. The year 1346 would give us an earlier limit, but most of the arguments are in favour of 1349 (Hammerich, p. 1271; Flavigny, p. 2282) and it is then safer to adopt 1349 which is the last possible year. 22 Although lately S.  2 4 3 . THE HISTORY OF THE REGULA SALVATORIS 3.2. The 0 text The RS once conceived, St. Birgitta's next concern was how to have it authorized by the Pope. It is open to discussion when she took the first initiative, but there is no evidence that she did so while she was still living in Sweden (she left Sweden for Rome some time before 135o [section 3.1]) and perhaps it was not until the Pope, Urban V, had returned from Avignon to Rome in 1367 that she started her action. Urban V, however, was reluctant and, since the Council of Lyon (1274) had renewed an earlier prohibition on founding new orders, he could not confirm the RS as an independent monastic rule. Birgitta's efforts resulted in a bull, issued by Urban V on the 5th of August 1370, giving her the following permission. She was allowed to found one monastery for nuns and one for monks at Vadstena. These monasteries were to be subordinate to the Regula Augustini and also to obey certain constitutions given in the bull. These constitutions are a heavily revised and abbreviated version of the RS. Now, the 0 text in the present edition consists of the bull of Urban V; the constitutions in the bull, i.e. the revised RS text, extend over §§ 319-357 of the 0 text and the parts of the bull surrounding the constitutions, which have been printed in smaller type, comprise §§ 318a—q and 358a—d. It is apparent from the medieval sources that St. Birgitta tried hard to obtain approval of the RS from the Pope (RS H 288-317; Rev. IV 137 [= Ex. 44]; Rev. VIII 51 [= Ex. 42]; Ex. 8). The Pope, Urban V, returned from Avignon to Rome on the i6th of October 1367 and stayed in Italy until the i 7th of April 1370, when he left again for Avignon (Höjer, pp. 5g f. with further references). If Birgitta did not get into touch with the Pope on the matter of approval before his return to Rome—and there is no evidence that she did (for the unproved attempt by Bishop Hemming, see section 3.1 above)— she must have called on him on one or more occasions during the period October 1367 to April 1370. Höjer (p. 59) and Lindblom (p. 39) say that she did so immediately on the arrival of the Pope in 1367, but there seems to be no support for this assumption in the mediasval sources, except for a dubious entry in the Diary of Vadstena.24 Wess&I (p. 65) assumes that it was not until 1368. The precise course of events has not yet been discovered. 1347 and 1348-1349 possible (pp. 173 f.). A recently discovered letter from King Magnus of Sweden to the Kings of France and England (Cambridge, Corpus Christi College, MS 404 fol. IO2v-I031 is clearly in favour of the later period, in fact, it seems almost decisive (for the letter and its implications, see E. Colledge, Epistola solitarii ad reges, Alphonse of Pecha as Organizer of Birgittine and Urbanist Propaganda [Medieval Studies 18, Toronto 1956, pp. 19- 49] pp• 32 f.; S. Stolpe, Birgitta och politiken [Opuscula ecclesiastica, Studier tillägnade G. Rosendal, Uppsala 1972, pp. 359-373; the letter is printed on pp. 371-3731; J. Liedgren, Två Birgittinska data, Biskop Hemmings resa för medling i hundraårskriget [Personhistorisk tidskrift 70, 1974, pp. 52-54D. 24 Diarium Vadzstenense (CCSMA 16, p. 7) for the year 1367: beata birgitta loquebatur rome tam pape quam imperatori. Since one part of the statement is erroneous (the Emperor Charles did not arrive in Rome until 1368), the other should not be trusted too far (cf. Steffen, p. XXIIP). 
 
 
 
 
 3.3. THE E TEXT 25 It is also apparent from the medieval sources that Birgitta tried to influence the Pope through the Emperor Charles (Rev. VIII 51 [= Ex. 42]; Ex. 8). It is generally held that these attempts took place after the arrival of Charles in Italy on the uth of October 1368 (cf. Höjer, p. 6o; Steffen, p. XXIV; Wess&i, pp. 65 f.; Lindblom, p. 39; Sun&n, pp. 137 ff). Now, the Pope left Rome for Avignon on the i7th of April 1370 but stayed in Monte- fiascone during the summer. Höjer (pp. 6o f.) has investigated the events in Monte- fiascone and I refer to him for the following account and for the medieval sources.25 Birgitta spent three months in Montefiascone, accompanied by her confessor, Alphonso of Ja&I, and supported by her friend Nicolaus Orsini, Count of Nola. The Count several times brought the subject of the RS up with the Pope and was finally entrusted, to- gether with some other pious men, with the task of examining the RS and reporting back to the Pope. Having corrected the Latin, which did not match the standards of the Papal chancellery, but leaving the contents unchanged, the Count received St. Birgitta's acknowledgement of the alterations and handed the RS over to the Pope for his approval. The Pope, however, had the RS further revised by a committee chaired by the Cardinal of Jerusalem and after this second revision he issued the bull of the 5th of August 1370 (= the 0 text of this edition, in which §§ 31 g-357 form the heavily revised RS text). 3.3. The E text St. Birgitta was not satisfied with the bull of Urban V (the 0 text) but was unable to obtain any change before her death in 1373. Her daughter, Katarina, however, continued the work and after several complications the Pope, Urban VI, issued a bull on the 3rd of December 1378 in which he approved of the RS. It was still only classified as constitutions subordinate to the Regula Augustini, not as a monastic rule of its own, but its appearance was much more similar to St. Birgitta's own text (II) than were the constitutions approved by Urban V (0). The E text of the present edition is the bull of Urban VI, which is built up in the following way. (a) In the centre there are the constitutions, i.e. the revised RS text (§§ 47-267). (b) They are enclosed in a letter from Cardinal Elziarius, of Sabrano, to whom was entrusted the task of examining the RS (see below), to the Pope, in which Elziarius recommends approval (§§ 46a—k and 268; printed in smaller type). (c) To the letter of Elziarius is attached a 25 To the medieval sources described by Höjer may be added a biography of Urban V dating from the i4th century (Vitae paparum Avionensium I ed. S. Hec domina a puericia reuelaciones diuinas habuit et claruit miraculis in vita et post mortem. The biography was written by Werner, canon of Bonn (dead in 1384), probably some time between 1376 and 1384 (G.  26 3. THE HISTORY OF THE REGULA SALVATORIS certificate from his scribe (§§ 269a—b; printed in smaller type). (d) The letter of Elziarius, with the RS and the certificate from his scribe, is finally embedded in the text of Urban VI (§§ 45a—k and 270; printed in smaller type). The developments after 1370 have been investigated by Höjer (pp. 65 f.) and I refer to him for the following account and for the medieval sources (cf. also RS 45a-46k). St. Birgitta was not satisfied with the 0 text and instructed her daughter, Katarina, to work for a renewed confirmation of the RS. Having left Rome for Sweden in connection with her mother's death in 1373, Katarina returned in 1375 and, together with Birgitta's confessors, Prior Petrus and Magister Petrus, submitted the RS to Pope Gregory XI. The RS (as weil as St. Birgitta's other revelations) was handed over to a committee to be examined and the committee recommended that it should be confirmed but the proce- durs was interrupted by the death of Gregory XI in the spring of 1378. Katarina, to- gether with Prior Petrus and the Vadstena brothers, Andreas Olaui and Magnus Petri, approached his successor, Urban VI, who handed the question over to a new committee. This committee too recommended a confirmation, but Urban VI had the RS examined once more by Cardinal Elziarius, of Sabrano, upon whose recommendation he issued the confirmatory bull (the E text). The date of this bull has been the subject of discussion (cf. the excursus of Höjer, pp• 354 if. [with further references]). The date of the bull itself is the 3rd of December 1378 (RS E 270 tercio nonas Decembris pontificatus nostri [sc. Vrbani VI] anno primo), whereas the letter of Elziarius is dated the 3rd of December 1379 (RS 268 sub anno dominice incarnacionis millesimo trecentesimo septuagesimo nono, tercia die mensis Decem- bris, secunda indiccione, pontificatus sanctissimi in Christo patris et domini nostri domini Vrbani, diuina prouidencia pape sexti, anno primo). This implies a contradiction, since the letter of Elziarius cannot, of course, be a year later than the bull. Höjer points out that the year 1379 is not simply a miswriting of Elziarius's scribe, since the information secunda indiccione fits with 1379, not with 1378. Furthermore, the Diary of Vadstena (CCSMA 16, p. g) gives the year 1379 (yet, a doubtful abbreviation renders the entire entry dubious) and this date is also found in the incipit of the Qe MS." There seem to be two possibilities: the bull was issued on the 3rd of December in either 1378 or 1379. Höjer (pp. 354 if.) assumed that 1378 was the correct year (especially since the bull was issued apud sanctam Mariam); the date in Elziarius's letter was simply due to the fact that Elziarius, who was from Naples, used the Neapolitan way of dating and the statements in the Diary of Vadstena and the Qe MS were errors due to the date in Elziarius's letter.27 The 3rd of December 1378 is most likely to be the correct date. Höjer (p. 355) refers to the incipit in Brockie's edition, which is based on the Qe MS (cf. Montag, pp. 1302 and 1471). The incipit in Qe (and Brockie's edition) runs thus: incipit regula sancti saluatoris a sede apostolica pro constitucionibus approbata anno domini 1379 per vrbanum VI tercia mensis decembris. The incipit appears in different forms in the Ue Qe Ge Ze Rea MSS and was accordingly composed in x (see the stemma on p. 3o). The year 1379, however, is only in Qe (the other MSS have no dates at all in the incipit), so the year 1379 was entered when Qe (or some lost ancestor of Qe) was written. 27 Höjer (pp. 355 f.), referring to Brockie (p. 103), says that the MS used by Brockie (Qe) was a gift from Vadstena to Gnadenberg and accordingly assumes, that the information on 1379, both in the Qe incipit and in the Diary of Vadstena, originates from one and the same tradition in Vadstena; this tradition depends on the date of Elziarius's letter. There is, however, no evidence whatsoever that Qe originates from Vadstena (in fact, Brockie does not say that it is a gift from Vadstena) and, consequently, the statements in the incipit of Qe and the Diary of 26

 28 3. THE HISTORY OF THE REGULA SALVATORIS There are only vague indications as regards the date and place of origin of the 1 text. It was certainly composed after 1378 (the date of the E text) and before 1496 (the date of the Oe MS). Montag, who can take most of the credit for clearly demonstrating that the b text is a separate version, found that a German translation of the cl) text was made in Gnadenberg in 1433 at the latest (one German MS, m 2, dates from that year, see Montag, pp. 134 f. and 57 ff.), which renders the period 1378-1433 probable as regards the origin of the Latin IN text. The place of origin is still more dubious; Montag (pp. 139 ff.) thinks that the Latin D text was transferred from Paradiso in Italy (it is open to doubt whether it was composed in Paradiso or not) to Gnadenberg in Germany. This assumption, however, is based only on the fact that almost all the Latin MSS of the D text are of German and Italian origin and that there were close connections be- tween Paradiso and Gnadenberg. 3.5. The A text The A text is a mixture of the II and E texts. The place of origin and the date are unknown (it was, of course, composed after 1378, the date of the E text). The A text is built up as follows. (a) It begins with the following paragraphs, which the compilator took from his H source (they are not in the E MSS): I, 2- II, 12, 13-21, 22-34 and 42-44. (b) There follows the central part of the RS, viz. §§ 48-267; here the A compilator had both a H and a E source and he paid more attention to his II source than did the 1 compilator (above). (c) Finally, there follow these paragraphs taken from the H source (they are not in the E MSS): 271-277, 278-287, 288-303 and 304-317. It is clear from this account that the A text may be described as a normal n text with some features specific to the E text intermingled in the central part (§§ 48-267). The A text has not been edited here because it is very similar to the H text and, being known from two MSS (Aaf3 and Ee) only, seems to have been of less importance than the (I) text. 3.6. The 'P text Montag (p. 1293) mentions that a late MS from Munich (Clm 27117 = we), dating from 1673, contains "die approbierte Version" (= the E text) in a form reworked to suit monks instead of nuns. This text has been termed II and it seems to be a revision into the "masculine" form based entirely on a E MS showing no influence from any of the H, 0, D or A texts. The late dating of the we MS would render the 'P text less interesting, but I have found that one chapter of it, viz. §§ 185-191, appears in the 0 o MSS (= 0 and oo, see notes and i i), as weil as in the Ces MS (note 3). Since the 0 o Ce MSS date from the i5th century, it is obvious that at least §§ 181-191 of the 'P text existed by then29 (it was, of course, composed after 1378, the date of the E text). The 29 It is likely that not only §§ 185-191 but also larger parts, maybe the entire Ii text, existed as early as the ,5th century, since there seem to be traces also of other parts in the Ce MS

 4. The manuscripts Most of the MSS which contain the RS are not used in the parts of the Birgitta corpus previously edited and the few already familiar MSS here form new configurations. This rendered it impossible to use previous research and indeed made it necessary to establish an entirely new stemma codicum. On some occasions the precise relations between the MSS in this stemma cannot be traced (for example, within the group), owing to the extensive contamination, which is a characteristic feature of the RS tradition. The existence of several fairly similar versions seems to have invited contamination, which, accordingly, can be traced not only between MSS of the same text version but also fre- quently between MSS of different versions.3° This is an obstacle, but, on the other hand, the work is facilitated by the existence of certificates in some of the MSS, describing their origin. In investigating the RS tradition, all the MSS listed in section 2.1 were exa- mined and arranged in a stemma diagram.31 In the following description, how- ever, those MSS have been left aside which are extremely fragmentary and have turned out to contribute neither to the stemma diagram nor to the establishment of the text, viz. F Qa Aaa Ceµ Cev Ler Tea Tes Te& wer3 Xeci. Lfa Nf Ofa b. bo MSS. The fragmentary MSS Qr3 Tey Er Rep sep Opp - y oo have proved more valuable and are, accordingly, included in the stemma (Ef Rep Sep are also used in establishing parts of the 4 text). MSS of a later date than AD i600 have been left aside, unless they have proved especially valuable in some respect; thus the Af Dr Ff Gf Hf Lfp Mf. Rf Uf. MSS have not been used below, 3° The extensive correcting of lost MSS, which lies behind the contamination, has a counter- part in the extant MSS. Most of them have corrections which have apparently been taken from other MSS (often belonging to some other version of the text) and the copyists often corrected the most trifling details, such as orthography, along with more substantial corrections. A contri- butory factor to this activity was probably that some copyists may have learnt the RS more or less by heart, since it was read aloud in the monasteries (such instructions are occasionally given together with the RS text; cf. also the regulation edited by Klemming [5, pp. 127-139], p. 137: ok läti läsa re gula saluatoris hwan maanadh and [concerning the 0 text] § 356). Knowledge acquired by memorizing might to some extent explain why sometimes only a few passages are subject to correction in some MSS (for example, the contamination of the Cea and Ae texts [subsections 4.2.1 and 4.2.4] or the isolated corrections entered by a second hand in P from a E MS [for example, the addition of nisi abbatisse in § 75, see the editions of the II and E texts]; cf. also the headings in Ke dealt with in subsection 4.2.5). The different instances of contamination have been traced as far as possible in the discussions below and are marked in the stemma diagram. Corrections in extant MSS are not marked in the stemma (but are always mentioned in the quotations and in the apparatus of the texts). 31 It would probably be possible to track down in different libraries a few more RS MSS than those listed in section 2.1. I think it unlikely, however, that such MSS would influence the establishment of the text and, since a continued search would only cause delay, without pro- ducing any benefit to match the expense, I have contented myself with the MSS listed in section 2.1. 
 
 
 
 
 32 4. THE MANUSCRIPTS whereas Wea Kf Mfo Ufo are utilized in the stemma (Kf also in establishing the E text). The Leo MS, finally, which has a peculiar and incomplete text, has been left aside. The worthless MSS having been cleared away, there remain 64 MSS of greater value, which form the stemma diagram. The stemma will be described from the top downwards. The relationship between the SI II E and OSAa groups is discussed first (section 4.0 and then the interna! relations within the II (section 4.2 —subsection 4.2.5) and the E (sec- tion 4.3—subsection 4.3.8) groups are considered, since they form the two main branches of the stemma. After that the (1) A ‘11 and 0 groups are discussed (section 4.4—subsection 4.9.6). Here I wish to add some technical remarks concerning the following pages. The SI II E b A '1' versions are dealt with first (section 4. i—section 4.7). From the stemmatic point of view, they are intermingled with and dependent on each other. Thus, in dealing with the internal relations in one of them, it is very often necessary to consider the readings in one or more of the other versions as well. This may easily be done, since one and the same paragraph number, preceding each example in section 4. i—section 4.7 refers to all six versions32 and since in each example I have accounted for all MSS of the H E sito A If texts.33 In doing this it has been necessary to adopt a special method. The normal procedure in describing errors used to construct a stemma is to quote the erroneous reading(s), to enumerate the MS(S) which have this reading and to quote the correct reading. The reader is free to draw the conclusion e silentio that all MSS not mentioned have the correct reading. This simple method cannot be applied to the S II E4bAT texts, as some of these version(s) or some MS(S) of some version(s) lack certain parts of the RS text which the other version(s) or MS(S) have. It has proved necessary also to account for those MSS which have the correct reading, in order to enable the reader to judge the errors properly. Thus, I give the error together with the MS(S) which have the error, followed by the words "instead of" and the correct reading together with an account of all the other MSS of all five versions, for example, 5 swecie K K° instead of norwegie II (exc. K Ke) D (nouergie Pe Cfa Fe des. Ef Se9 A des. E IP." On some occasions there may be more than one correct reading, since the archetypes of thellEOAT texts may diller and a reading of the archetype E, for example, is quite as correct as a reading of the archetype D. The 0 text, finally, which is dealt with in section 4.8—subsection 4.9.6, is so different from the S2 II E (I) A and I' texts that a comparison with them is meaningless. As regards the term "error" it should be observed that a reading may be classified as an error either for syntactical/contextual reasons or on account of the distribution of the reading in the stemma (sometimes both types of criteria apply; for further discussion, see Eklund, SA, pp. 23 f.). The printed H E cto texts have the same paragraph numbers (subsection 2.4.1) and these numbers are applicable also to the (2 A I' texts, which have not been edited. n The n text does not have any MSS of its own; it is reconstructed from the II and E texts [section 5.1). 34 The following abbreviations are used in such accounts: "exc." (= excepto, exceptis) denotes that some MS(S) have a different reading, which is given somewhere else in the account; "deest", "des." (= desunt) indicates that some MS(S) give no information at all, either because a major part of the text, to which part the passage under discussion belongs, is missing in them or 32

The II (A) reading is inferior to the E (021) reading, since the reference of tale is unclear. Now, Fig. i explains this passage without any difficulties. The lost n MS had the words quod licite laborare possint tunc and via E they were handed down to the E MSS; in II, however, they dropped out, which brought about the inferior reading, and are accordingly missing in the II MSS; the OSA. MS is then to be connected somewhere above H and E (maybe even above SI, if there was some lost MS[S] in that position). Thus, according to Fig. i, it is merely a trifling omission in II. Adopting Fig. 2, one must assume the existence of a lost ancestor of OSA. and E in which the words quod ... tunc were added, since they were lacking in the II MS itself, according to the testimony of the II MSS. This in turn means that the H reading, which is inferior from the point of content, is the original one (veritten by Prior Petrus when he translated the RS).36 Such an explanation is more complicated than the one according to Fig. i, as it involves two steps: Prior Petrus expressed himself indistinctly and he was corrected by an addition later on in the tradition." The decisive factor, however, is the frequency of errors specific to the lost (p MS and Cea respectively and the character of these errors. The 9 MS apparently had very few errors of its own (i.e. those adduced in establishing the 9 group above), one of which is an omission. The Cea MS has about 20 errors of its own, nine of which are omissions. All these 20 errors, however, have been corrected, many of them probably by the first hand.45 In these circumstances, it is unlikely that the 9 Cea readings in, §§ 128, 142, 144, Igo and io6 would have arisen independently in 9 and Cea and ac- cordingly they mean that Fig. 4 must be reconsidered. Apart from demonstrating that L 130 Ae form a group (which has already been established in subsection 4.2.3) these two types of errors indicate either that the L text was contaminated from outside the g group (the L pf3 errors then arose in an ancestor common to L pr3 and the p0 Ae errors were in g, but L offers the correct text through contamination) or that the Ae text was contaminated from outside the group (according to analogous reasoning). In fact, both the L text and the Ae text are contaminated. As regards the L text, two passages have already been given (§§ 61 and 81 in note 52) in which L offers the correct reading, although an error arose somewhere above Q. The contamination of the L text originates from a MS belonging to the E or the D group, since L has readings in common with these groups in some passages, for example, 169 contra L E (deest Q9 D (des. RO SO Tel 'P (des. 00 00 Ce5) extra II (exc. L deest p7) A; 267 cogitent L (and Kel) E (cogitant V Ca deest Q0) (D (des. Ef ReR SO Tel (des. 00 0° Ceö) legant II (exc. L Kel deest p7) A. The exact position of this second source of the L text cannot be traced.55 The contamination of the Ae text has also already been exemplified by errors which arose above (§ 220 [subsection 4.2.1] and io6 in note 44). The location of the second source of Ae is dubious. There was apparently some influence from a I MS, as is clear from this heading: 154 de habitu fratrum et sacerdotum Ae E (exc. x de habitu sacerdotum et fratrum He Pa Xe0 [and Ke, see subsection 4.2.5] 13f de habitu fratrum Ca Lee Up Jf de vestimentis sacerdotum et fratrum Oa oa deest Q0) Iee '1' (des. 00 o1 Ce9 de habitu omnium fratrum x CD (exc. Neither L nor Ae can be identical with Q since IP cannot be subordinate to any of them (one objection to this idea is that both L and Ae have errors of their own). 
 
 
 
 
 4.2. THE RELATIONS WITHIN THE II GROUP 43 4.2.5. The relationship between t pY K Ke P. In this subsection the Ke MS must first be considered alone. After that the relations between t K Ke P are established, whereas the connection of pY is discussed towards the end, since pY is very fragmentary. The RS text in Ke may be divided into four sections with regard to changes in the handwriting (§§ 5-78, 79-136, 137-196 and 197-277). The transitions always occur in the middle of pages, so they are not to be explained by the assumption that the leaves were brought together from different MSS. Even if different scribes are respon- sible for each or at least some of these parts, there is, accordingly, no reason for assuming that they used different originals, especially since such an assumption has no support in the distribution of the errors (they show the same pattern in all four sections). Thus, Ke may be handled just like the other n MSS, irrespective of the changes in the hand- writing. The headings in Ke, however, require some comments, since they seem to imply contamination from a E MS." Many of them are left out, but the following are not: 65 de horis; 73 de silencio; 79 quod nulli ingrediantur monasterium; 86 de ieiuniis; 96 de modo recepcionis monialium; 148 de numero sororum clericorum et eorum seruitorum; 154 de habitu sacerdotum et fratrum; 171 de sacerdotum officio; 179 de communione scilicet quibus diebus sorores communicare debeant; 146 quod non dona accipiantur; 197-199 de construccione monasterii; 232 vt sorores non sint ociose; 244-245 de confessionibus. The one in § 73 is identical to the E heading and several of the others are so similar to the E headings that it can hardly be due to pure coincidence. It is not necessary, however, to suppose that a E MS was checked; the headings may have been composed by someone who had reminiscences of the E headings stored in his memory (cf. note 3o). This in- fluence from E has been marked in the stemma (and in Figs. 10-13 below) but there is no evidence that it changed anything in the Ke text other than the headings. Thus, it has no implications for the subsequent discussion on the relations between n K Ke P.  4.3. THE RELATIONS WITHIN THE E GROUP 45 MS63 were corrected through the contamination of the K and Ke texts; since the conta- mination of the Ke text, as stated above, can be proved only in the headings (and there are P errors in the other parts as well), it is safer not to assume that P is an ancestor of K Ke. As regards the relations between K and Ke, K can hardly in itself be an ancestor of Ke, among other things, owing to the Ke P errors in §§ 55 and 59 (above). Nor is Ke likely to be an ancestor of K, since Ke lacks many of the headings as weil as paragraphs 225-228 and 278-317 (although they could have been introduced from another source when the lost K ancestor was corrected, there is no evidence for this in these parts of the text). Accordingly, it seemed safest to assume that K and Ke derive from one and the same lost MS, as shown in the stemma diagram. There remains p7, which was linked to the n group already by 18 noui palmites (subsec- tion 4.2.3). Being very fragmentary, p? has only §§ 5-34 and 273-277 in common with K Ke P (Ke lacks §§ 271-272 and 278-317). Within these paragraphs, p7 has some errors in common with K Ke (in §§ 278-317 also many errors in common with K), for example, g multique alii p? Since 137 shows no affinity with P, it has been taken together with the K Ke branch. It cannot be subordinate to the K Ke ancestor, since K Ke have errors which are not in 137, for example, 5 swecie above, and has, accordingly, been connected at a point between n and the K Ke ancestor. 4.3. The relations within the E group The E group dillers from the H group, the E ramification being made up not only of two but of several branches. The existence of some of these, viz. the a branch, the ö branch and the g branch is proved by certificates preserved in the extant MSS, which also render it possible to identify certain lost MSS, viz. a Pyö and g. In a manifold ramification, the establishment of the text is generally simple and the established text highly reliable. A prerequisite condition, however, is that the different branches are entirely independent of each other and of sources outside the group. In the E group, unfortunately, the a branch and the g branch are both contaminated from a II MS, which renders the establishment of the text more complicated than it would otherwise be. The relations within the E group are established and described in detail in subsec- tions 4.3.1 - 4.3.8 in smaller type below. writing and this dating was adopted by Kraft (pp. 78 f.), whereas P. Diedrichsen (Texthistoriske bemaerkninger til Reuelaciones sancte Birgitte [APS I°, Copenhagen 1935-1936, pp. 232-249], pp. 245 f.) tried to prove an earlier date, viz. about 1390 (but Diedrichsen's argumentation on the similarity between the K MS and the Munich MS Clm. 26647 does not seem conclusive for the dating of K). As regards Ke, finally, T. Nyberg (Skriftföljder och lägg 2 [NTBB 58, 1971, pp. 17-33], pp. 18-2o) argues that the volume was put together in the middle of the i5th century (after 1462) but that the leaves containing the RS text may be considerably older than that (ibid., p. 22). 63 Examples of P errors: 115 omni P instead of in omni II (exc.  46 4. THE MANUSCRIPTS 4.3.1. The relationship between E a R y if sea He pa xef3 Ca. I start by establishing the relations between EaDy If Sea, since they are based mainly on certificates in the MSS, taking the He pa xe0 group and the Ca MS into consideration towards the end of the subsection. The P and Sea MSS are linked together by three certificates in them, 64 which are, so to speak, similar to boxes placed inside each other in the following way. The bull of Urban VI (the E text, i.e. §§ 45a-27o) is surrounded by a certificate issued by Bishop Knut Bosson, of Linköping, i.e. the first part of the certificate is placed before the bull and the second part after it. Bishop Knut states that, at the request of the con- fessor-general and some other brothers from Vadstena, he supervised the copying of the authentic bull of Urban VI kept at Vadstena (E); 65 the copy was to be sent to other monasteries abroad and the procedure took place at Vadstena on the i 4th of July 1428. The copy has been called a. This certificate is in tum surrounded by another similar one, issued by Bishop Nicolaus Böddeker, of Schwerin, at the request of the confessor-general Jacob Ropersdorf, of Marienkron, in which Bishop Nicolaus guarantees the authenticity of a copy produced directly from Bishop Knut's copy. The procedure took place on the 2oth of June in 1446 and the copy has been called P.66 There is a third similar certi- ficate surrounding the others in which Archbishop Dietrich, of Cologne, also at the request of Jacob Ropersdorf, guarantees the authenticity of a copy made from 13; the 64 The certificates are also in the Ff Gf Lft3 Mfa Ufa MSS, which accordingly belong together with If Sea; they are, however, not included in the discussions, since they have proved worthless (section 4); at least Mfa Ufa and probably also Ff Gf VO, are descended from P. The certificates (together with those discussed in subsection 4.9.3) are dealt with by Nyberg in Kloster- griindungen, pp. 231 ff. 65 There is no doubt that the bull at Vadstena is the authentic bull (E), since it is described thus by Bishop Knut: Vniuersitatis vestre noticie constare volumus ... nos litteras apostolicas nec non regulam et constituciones ordinis Vrbani pape sexti, in se litteram domini Elziarii sanctarum Marie virginis et Birgitte, Sancti Saluatoris nuncupati, continentes, sua vero bulla plumbea in filis sericis pendente iuxta morem Romane curie bullatas, non rasas, non cancellatas nec abolitas vidisse, nec in aliqua sui parte suspectas seu viciatas sed omni prorsus vicio et suspicione carentes inspexisse et diligenter perlegisse. Bishop Knut's clerk, who also testifies in the certificate, says that he did not carry out the copying himself, since he was prevented by other tasks and it is not said who did the copying. The reason for this may be that the copy already existed when Bishop Knut arrived at Vadstena, but there is no reason for assuming that the copy was not produced directly from E. Under all circumstances, it is evident from the certificate that it was carefully checked against the genuine E MS. The passage discussed from the statement of the presens interfui ideo presentes presentacioni scribe runs thus: quia premissarum litterarum transsumpcionis litteras seu presens transsumptum ex hiis de verbo ad verbum, nichil, quod sensum vel effectum earundem immutat vel divariat, apponendo vel deponendo, de mandato domini mei episcopi prefati transsumpsi et in hanc publicam formam de alterius manu, me aliis certis negociis signo et nomine meis Kanuti impedito, transscriptas redegi, quam vna cum appensione sigilli solitis et consuetis signaui. 66 It is emphasized in the certificate that Bishop Nicolaus, his clerk and the witnesses had access to and compared Bishop Knut's authentic copy (a), the seal of which is accurately described. Bishop Nicolaus's clerk, however, says that the copy on which he is testifying (p) was written not by him but by someone else (not specified), which may imply that the copying had already been made on behalf of Jakob Ropersdorf when he called on Bishop Nicolaus (cf. note 65). Yet there is no reason for doubting that p was copied directly from a (without any intermediate stages) and it is clear from the certificate that it was carefully checked against the a MS.

There is, however, yet another certificate of this type flanking the others in the P MS, in which Archbishop Dietrich, of Cologne, certifies on a new copy produced directly from his own copy of 1451; the procedure took place at the request of the syndic, Wilhelm van Rekelinchusen, from the monastery at Kampen (on the IJssl Meer). The certificate is dated the 28th of July 1459 and the new copy is apparently identical with the extant If MS. The If MS is written on parchment and contains only the bull of Urban VI with the certificates. It is written throughout in one and the same (extremely clear and legible) handwriting, except for the statements of the two clerks which conclude bishop Dietrich's certificate of 1459. These two statements are in two cursive handwritings differing from the preceding part of the MS and from each other. The only reasonable explanation is that If is the actual copy produced in 1459 and the two statements by the clerks their original statements in their own handwritings (their emblems also appear under their statements). Thus, the If MS is derived directly from y. The Sea MS cannot be identical with y for different reasons. According to the certificate of Bishop Dietrich dated 1451, the y MS contained only the bull of Urban VI and the certificates written in six folios and a half (according to If; Sea says ten folios68). The passage from § 159 may perhaps be due to coincidence, whereas this is highly improbable in the cases from §§ 249 and 251.75 The simplest explanation of these passages is that E had the readings offered by Kf BO (Dec') and the most important of the g MSS and that the If Sea reading was introduced through contamination from a H MS, as shown in the stemma diagram. 76 The contamina- 73 The Ca MS also has some sporadic features in common with k MSS (for example, eundem in § 71 [subsection 4.3.5]); the Ca text was probably contaminated on more than one occasion. 74 The fact that also Dea Bf Ge 3 have the y H readings in § 159 is probably due to contamination (there is extensive contamination in the x and 3 groups [subsections 4.3.7 and 4.3.8] and probably also in the Bf text [subsection 4.3.5]; besides, the positions of Dea and Bf are dubious [subsections 4.3.4 and 4.3.5]). 75 The case in § 15g is probably not accidental, since the addition of a coordinating conjunction is extremely rare in If Sea (subsection 5.2.5). The opposite explanation, viz. that E had et de penis and et dropped out independently on at least three occasions, is unlikely, since coordina- ting conjunctions are comparatively seldom missing in Kf Bet V (subsection 5.2.5). In the passages from §§ 249 and 251, three conjunctions following shortly after each other are changed. If E had the Kf Bd V readings, it seems out of the question that y would have got the conjunctions of the II text by mere coincidence (changes of conjunctions seldom occur in If Sea [cf. the account of et and ac in connection with 45g in subsection 5.2.4]). If E had the H If Sea readings, the Kf BO 4 readings could not possibly have arisen on at least three occasions independently (cf. the same account of et and ac in subsection 5.2.4). Accordingly steps must be taken in the stemma to cope with these passages. 76 The opposite assumption, viz. that E had the If Sea H readings, would render it very hard to explain the consensus of Kf Bet (Dea) and (the most important of) the 4 MSS and would ne- cessitate the introduction of not only one but at least two instances of contamination. Ac- cordingly, this solution has been rejected. One could, however, argue against the solution chosen in the stemma from an investigation of the practice of the E revisers. A comparison of the E text with the H text reveals that the revisers responsible for E (a) never created an asyndetic co-ordination by the removal of a conjunction (or vice versa by the addition of a conjunction) or (b) changed coordinating conjunctions, such as et, ac, nec, seu, vel or aut. Under these cir- cumstances, one could argue that the E revisers retained the readings offered by the H MSS in. the E text in the passages from §§ 159, 249 and 251, which in turn would argue against the solu- tion adopted in the stemma diagram. This argument is rendered useless by the fact that we do not know for certain the readings of the actual n MS used by the E revisers; the readings of Kf BO (Dea) and (the most important of) the MSS may have already arisen as errors in this SI MS, in which case the E revisers, following their current practice, incorporated them in the E text and then there is no opposition to the solution chosen for the stemma (cf. note 137). —Objections based on judgements as to the improbability of corrections having been entered in the more or less official documents which constitute the series of copies from E down to If are not particularly valid. 4 - Sten Eklund 
 
 
 
 
 50 4. THE MANUSCRIPTS tion from n has been marked by an arrow pointing to y, but in reality the corrections may have been entered anywhere between E and y. 4.3.2. The relationship between E ö Kf. The le MS is a late MS (1657) but, is, ne- vertheless, of very great value, since it represents an independent line down from E. This appears from a certificate on fol. 7r—', in which Bishop Henrik, of Linköping, testifies that some bulls and other documents which follow the certificate were copied from the originals in Vadstena; among them is the bull of Urban VI with the RS. The copy pro- duced on this occasion, which has been called ö, is, accordingly, derived directly from £." The procedure took place in the bishop's residence at Linköping at the request of two brothers, Johannes de Oost and Johannes de Elst, from the monastery of Ma- rienthron at Dendermonde in Flanders. The copy bears no date but the only Henrik, Bishop of Linköping, who fits chronologically is Bishop Henrik Tidemansson (1465-- 1500).78 The Kf MS is derived from ö (there may, of course, have been some lost inter- mediate MS between ö and Kr). 4.3.3. The relationship between E B. The Be MS is the so - called Liber priuilegiorum monasterii Vadstenensis, a collection of documents concerning privileges granted to the monastery at Vadstena by bulls or otherwise. The original documents were preserved in caskets in the monastery and the Be MS was composed in order to save the originals from being worn out by too frequent handling (this motive is given at the beginning of the MS; cf. E. Nygren, CCSMA i i, p. XI). In these circumstances it would be reasonable to assume that Be0 was copied directly from the authentic bull which was preserved in one of the caskets; and since there is no definite obstacle to this idea, it has been accepted in the stemma diagram. There are several corrections in Be0. Some of them were entered by the original scribe, others on some later occasion. The latter have been designated Be02 in the apparatus, but in reality there seem to have been at least two correctors. One of them wrote in a pale-grey ink (for example, in the middle of fol. 43v: the picture in the margin, the addition varat in bulla [§ 47] and the addition of -bus in successoribus [§ 46k]), the other in a very dark ink (for example, in fol. 46r: all the explanations of the Trinitarian formula In nomine Patris et Filii et Spiritus Sancti [cf. note 162]). These two correctors have not been distinguished in the apparatus (both are called Be02), since such a distinction is generally of no importance as regards the establishment of the text and it sometimes is " According to the certificate certain bulls of Urban VI, Martin V, Boniface IX and Gregory XI, a letter of Bishop Thomas, of London, and a decree of the Garnera Apostolica were copied from the originals (as regards the hulls, the authenticity is emphasized thus: litteras quasdam ... Vrbani sexti, Martini quinti, Bonifacii noni, Gregorii vndecimi cum filis sericis eorumque veris bullis plumbeis more Romane curiae bullatas). The certificate occupies fol. 7r -v, immediately after it there follow the bull of Urban VI with the RS (foll. 8r-23v), five other bulls of Martin, Urban and Boniface (foll. 24v-59r [24r is empty]), the letter of Bishop Thomas, of London (foll. 59v-631, and the decree of the Garnera Apostolica (foll. 63v-66v). After that there follow many other bulls (issued by Gregory, Urban, Boniface and Martin as weil as by other popes) and documents. It may be somewhat open to doubt how many of the bulls after fol. 66v were actually in ö, but there can hardly be any doubt that the bull of Urban VI with the RS was, since it follows immediately after the certificate and before the letter of Bishop Thomas. 78 The procedure probably did not take place until 1467 at the earliest. Bishop Henrik was elected in 1465, but in 1467 he had still not been appointed by the Pope and styles himself electus (J. A. Westerlund—J.A. Setterdahi, Linköpings stifts herdaminne i, Linköping 1919, pp. 35 f.); in the certificate he calls himself miseracione diuina episcopus Lincopensis.

In § gg, there is a passage which at first glance seems to imply that the Ben text is contaminated from a k MS: gg inquirat requirat (requirat exp.) Ben instead of inquirat H (deest pr) E (exc. Be' Qe Ue Ge Ze a) o E (in requirat corr. Iea deest Tel A '` (des. 00 0a Ce8) requirat Qe Ue Ge Ze (in inquirat corr. Ze) a D (exc. w E des. Rea Sea). The ap- pearance of inquirat requirat in the current BO text would normally indicate that an ancestor of Ben (a lost MS between E and Ben) had one of the two readings in the current text and the other one entered as a correction and that the Bea copyist erroneously took both words, which in turn would mean that the lost Bea ancestor had two sources, one of which was located among the k MSS (the distribution in § gg shows that the error requirat arose in k). A doser inspection of Bea reveals these facts: inquirat was written by the first hand but erased (or worn out) and filled in afterwards by the dark-ink corrector; - quirat of the following word requirat was written by the first hand, whereas re - was written afterwards in an erasure of two letters by the grey-ink (or maybe the dark-ink) corrector (in addition the entire word was expunged and crossed out by the dark-ink corrector). There may be different explanations, but, since re - is in an erasure, it is not to be taken for granted that the original BO scribe wrote inquirat requirat; he may have written inquirat inquirat.8° On the other hand, it would be curious if by mere accidence he doubled a word which was changed by error also in k. It would be somewhat odd that such an official MS as BO should offer a contaminated text (though it is apparent from several corrections in BO that the text was not sacrosanct). Although the passage from § gg provokes the suspicion that the Bea text was contaminated from a k source (corrections having been entered in a lost MS between E and Ben), it does not constitute sufficient evidence for such an assumption and no contamination has been marked in the stemma diagram. 4.3.4• The relationship between E Dea. The relationship between (I) and the H and E groups The (1) text is a mixture of the H and E texts: §§ 1-34, 42 - 44 and 271-317 are taken from a II MS (they are not in the E MSS); in §§ 48-267 the compilator used both a H and a E MS; §§ 35 - 41, finally, are from some other source (§§ 37 - 41 = Ex. 46). The H MS used by the b compilator has henceforth been called "the II source" and his E MS "the E source". The (I) tradition is heavily contaminated and, in dealing with the relations within it and, especially, in establishing the text, it is essential to know (a) that the II source belonged to the t group within the II branch and the E source to the group within the E branch, (b) that in §§ 48-267 the compilator created almost nothing of his own, preferred the E source to the H source and, in fact, followed the E source almost slavishly, except in passages concerning the status of the RS, in which he preferred the H readings, and (c) that in §§ 1-34, 42-44 and 2 7 1- 317 he felt more free to change the text or, which is perhaps more likely, his ri source had readings which are not preserved in any extant H MS. The reasons for these three statements are given in subsections 4.4.1-4.4.3 in smaller type below. 4.4.1. The location of the H and E sources. In this investigation, I have used only passages in which the reading of the lost 4) MS is clear beyond doubt from the con- sensus between the co MSS (Pe Cfa), the v MSS (Ne Oe and the fragmentary MSS Ef Re0 See, when they have the text) and Me; the readings of the E MSS are of no interest, since they are even more heavily contaminated than o) v Me and are of inferior value. The location of the II source can be traced only from an investigation of §§ 1-34, 42-44 and 271-317, since in §§ 48-267 the compilator took so few passages from his II source that there is no guidance as to its location. On some occasions, the 4) MSS agree with MSS from the n group of the II branch: 18 noui palmites (subsection 4.2.3); 34 et consurge CD (exc. E These passages may not seem absolutely convincing (there are a few more, but they are less conclusive), but they indicate that the H source was a lost t MS and, since there is nothing which seriously points to another location, this has been accepted.91 9 ° Owing to this contamination from a H MS, Ce0 has several features in common with the II text (as weil as with the E text). In this part of the text, the 01:0 compilator had both a ni and a E source; when they offered him different readings, he chose according to certain principles, which have been traced in the following way. All the passages have been collected in which 094 agrees with H MSS against all the E MSS95 and in which D agrees with E MSS against all the H MSS.96 In such passages, the i group (for which see subsection 4.2.1) than to the incipit of the cp group (the one printed in the H text), primarily the presence of the words ponitur primo, which are also in the i incipit but not in the (p incipit. On the other hand, the superlative form deuotissime is in both the 0 and cp incipits, whereas the i incipit has deuote. The similarity with the i incipit is by no means conclusive (the lost n MS used by the D compilator may have had a mixture of the ti and cp incipits) and there is also a positive indication that the H source was not a 'C MS in the passage from § 313 (subsection 4.2.4) in which (I) would probably have had aliis, not plus, under such circumstances. 92 There are some passages in which 0, to judge from the apparatus of the E text, agrees with other groups within E than 4, as in 85 hoc aliquociens (with If Sea), 212 exspoliasset (with Kf) and 227 fuerint necessarii (with Kf). In these passages, however, the 0 reading is also in some or most of the 1 MSS (in all three passages, Qe Ue have the 0 reading), owing to the extensive contamination in the 1 group (subsections 4.3.7-4.3.8), and, accordingly, these passages in reality do not point to another location of the E source. 93 The fact that Oa of the a group seems to be somewhat more reliable in this respect than Ge Ze Rea of the x group, although the lost E source apparently belonged to the x group, is probably due to the severe contamination of the Ge Ze Rea texts. 94 It was a prerequisite condition for the passages used that the D reading should be clear from the consensus between w v Me, as described at the beginning of subsection 4.4.1 above. 95 should agree with at least the ri group within the H branch (since the H source was a lost MS of the n group [above]), but in reality it turns out that 0 agrees with all the II MSS in passages of this type. 96 0 should agree with at least Qe Ue Oa of the E branch (since these are the best representatives of the lost E source [above]), but in most passages of this type 0 agrees with more (generally with all) E MSS. 
 
 
 
 
 4.4. THE RELATIONSHIP BETWEEN <D AND THE H ANDE GROUPS 57 the D compilator apparently had different readings in his H and E sources; in the former type he chose the reading of his H source and in the latter he preferred the E source. I have also brought together all the passages in which the reading of CD is in none of the H or E MSS; in these cases, the CD compilator created a text version of his own (or maybe the CD reading was in one of his sources without being preserved in any of the extant MSS). The investigation yielded the following results. (a) The CD compilator preferred the H readings in all the passages in which Christ appears speaking in the first person in the II text, thus retaining the character of direct revelation, which was removed in E by changes in these passages (cf. section 3 and note I26.)" They are about 25, for example, 88 natiuitatis mee II (deest p7) (D (des. Ef ReR sef3 Tel A natiuitatis domini E 'P (des. 00 00 C"); 141 corpus meum II (deest p7) cp (des. Ef Ref3e (b) The cp compilator preferred the n reading in § 92 by not adopting the addition et in vigilia ... profitentur, which was made in E in order to emphasize the position of the RS as subordinate to the Regula Augustini.98 The D compilator thus retained the status of the RS as an independent rule. The omission is hardly accidental, with regard to the similar passages listed in section (d) below. (c) The CD compilator preferred the E readings in all passages, except those specified under (a)—(b) above.99 There are about 13o such cases and they form a most hetero- geneous collection; examples may easily be gathered from a comparison between the H, E and (1) texts. (d) The (D compilator created almost nothing himself. I have noted ten occasions on which <D offers readings which are in none of the II or E MSS. In five passages of the type described in section (a) above, the CD compilator did not take exactly the H readings but created mixtures of the H and E texts, always sticking to his main principle of letting Christ appear in the first person. I give the (b readings (cf. the II and E texts): go virginis marie matris mee; 157 corporis mei; 167 virginis marie matris mee; 237 ego ipse dominus omnium; 241 regnum meum (post habere). On one occasion, he even over-elaborated by changing to the first person in a passage in which neither H nor E had the first person: 236 meo. These six passages cannot be said to indicate any initiative on the compilator's part. The principles of the CD compilator in §§ 1-34, 42-44 and 271-317. In these paragraphs, the (1) compilator had only his n source. There are several occasions on which the CD reading103 is not preserved in any extant II MS. This may suggest the idea that the CD compilator felt more free to re-arrange the text on his own initiative in these paragraphs than in §§ 48-267. On the other hand, there is no reason for such a distinction and there is another equally plausible explanation: the CD compilator followed his H source carefully, but this MS differed more from the extant ii MSS (to which group it belonged, subsection 4.4.1) than the E source from the Qe Ue Oa MSS. 100 In § 257, the (1) compilator retained the alteration of the E revisers in a similar passage: § 257 regula et constituciones iste. The explanation is probably that the expression does not definitely classify the RS as constitutions in the CD text: in the E text the word regula must refer to the Regula Augustini (since this is the only rule mentioned elsewhere in the E text) and constituciones to the RS; in the CD text, however, the word regula must refer to the RS itself, since it is designated "rule" in other passages in the CD text and since no other rule is mentioned elsewhere in the CD text. — The <D compilator retained the word constitucionum of the E text also in § 92. 1 °1 It should be observed that errors which arose in 2+. or x do not appear in the apparatus of the E text but in (1) and may give the false impression that the <D compilator created something on his own initiative. Such passages are: 78 singulariter (also in Be02, cf. subsection 4.3.6 [complete account] and 4.4.1); 189 et confessa non fuerit (and Be with non above the line); 264 de fossa (also in Be02). In reality, many of the k MSS (including Qe Ue, in all three passages) have the (1) readings, so they were certainly already in the CD compilator's E source. The case of 122 muniat is more doubtful in this respect, since ipsa is missing in 3 MSS only, not in Qe Ue (sub- section 4.3.6). 1 °2 The investigation clearly indicates that the Qe Ue Oa MSS, taken together, give a fairly good picture of the Co compilator's E source. In one passage only out of all those on which the investigation is based, viz. § 59 in section (d), is there reason for assuming that the E source may have had another reading than the one offered by the Qe Ue Oa MSS. This fact, in combina- tion with the principles of the CD compilator, as outlined in the investigation, constitutes a very useful tool in the hazardous tasks of building the (I) stemma and establishing the CD text. 1 °3 The CD reading being clear from the consensus between co v Me (cf. note 94). 
 
 
 
 
 4.5. THE RELATIONS WITHIN THE <D GROUP 59 4.5. The relations within the (1) group It is hard to specify the relations within the D group, owing to the extensive contamination involved, but the knowledge of the 11 and E sources used by the D compilator (subsection 4.4.1) and of the principles applied by him (subsections 4.4.2-4.4.3) is a good aid. Whenever the F MSS offer two (or more) different readings, this knowledge provides an opportunity to judge which of the two readings was actually in D, the other one being an error which arose somewhere below D. The investigation indicates that there are three groups, viz. co v and Me, which are roughly of the same value, and a fourth group, E, which is of much inferior value. The relations between the w v Me groups cannot be specified, whereas this is possible as regards the internal relations within the o and v groups (see the stemma diagram).  4.7. THE I' GROUP v 63 v v Ne Oe Er RO ser3 Ne Oe Ro So E' Ne Oe Er Re0 se0 Fig. 15 Fig. 16 Fig. 17 importance whatsoever, as regards the establishment of the text, since E' and the Re0 SO group can never be used in one and the same part of the text. The texts in these MSS were heavily contaminated on at least three, probably more occasions (Za, for example, offers the H headings through contamination). The four MSS have been brought together in a group without further specifications; the different instances of contamination are symbolized by one arrow pointing to the group. These MSS are completely useless for establishing the text. 4.6. The A group The A text is preserved in two MSS, viz. Mo and Ee. Since it did not seem worth while to edit the A text (section 3.5), I have not investigated in detail either the relations between A and the II and E groups or the internal relations in the A group.'" 4.7. The 'P group The entire masculine 'P text is preserved in the Wea MS, whereas the Of3 of3 Ces MSS contain only §§ 185-191.110 Th e we MS is late (1673), but, as I stated above (section 3.6), at least §§ 185-191 of the 'P text and probably also more paragraphs than these already existed in the i5th century. Since the 'P text is not to be edited in the present work (section 3.6), I have not examined the relations in detail, but it is obvious that the l iff text was produced from a E MS (probably of the y group, cf. note 69) without interference from the II group. 1 " A cursory inquiry, however, indicates that such investigations would yield some results. The H source of the A text, for example, seems to have been an n MS, cf. 18 noui palmites (subsection 4.2.3). It would be of some interest also to settle the question whether the A text is completely independent of the <b text or not. 11 ° There seem to be traces of the I' text also in other paragraphs than 185-191 in the Cef3 MS (cf. note 3).—Furthermore, it should be mentioned that §§ 185-191 in the Gr MS are primarily made up of E text but have been changed into 'Y text. 
 
 
 
 
 64 4. THE MANUSCRIPTS 4.8. The relationship between 0 and the g/ and H groups The 0 text was issued in 1370 (section 3.2). The E text did not exist then (section 3.3) and accordingly the (1) A and 'P texts did not exist either (sec- tions 3.4-3.6). This means that a MS with SI or H text formed the basis for the creation of the 0 text, but the precise relation of the 0 MS to the n and H MSS cannot be traced, since the 0 text has been completely reworked and re-arranged. The 0 MS may branch out above SI, be subordinate to SI (i.e. branch out directly from n, from some lost MS between n and H or from some lost MS between S/ and E) or even be subordinate to II (if the lost II MS originated before 1370). In the stemma diagram, 1 have just marked an un- specified connection with SI. 4.9. The relations within the 0 group The 0 ramification consists of five independent branches.11' The existence of three of them, viz. the p branch, the K branch and the tk branch, is proved by certificates copied in extant MSS; these certificates have also rendered it possible to identify certain lost MSS (the p a t n K ti) MSS). The Qf branch is represented by a file copy of the bull (q) produced and preserved in the papal chancellery. These conditions are extremely favourable to a reconstruc- tion of the 0 text and the 0 text is accordingly highly reliable. The relations within the 0 group are described in detail in subsections 4.9. 1-4.9.6 in smaller type below. 4.9.1. The relationship between 0 Qf. The sign of the Qf MS (Reg. Avin. 171) reveals that it belongs to the files of the Holy See from the last years of Urban V.112 There must have been a draft in the papal office (u) and either the bull (0) and the file copy (Qf) were both produced from this draft (Fig. 18) or the file copy was produced from the bull itself (0) before it was dispatched from the office (Fig. is). A definite decision on this issue seems not to be obtainable from historical facts, nor can it be gathered from errors in the MSS. Since the aim is to reconstruct the 0 text, not the hypothetical u draft, this ambiguity is of no importance; Qf certainly constitutes one line to 0 entirely separate from the lines of the other MSS and is, accordingly, of very great value. In the stemma diagram I have chosen Fig. 19. 4.9.2. The relationship between 0 Bea. The Be MS is the so-called Liber priuilegiorum monasterii Vadstenensis, a copy of documents concerning privileges granted to the monastery at Vadstena, composed in order to save the original documents from being worn out by too frequent handling. It is reasonable to assume that the texts in Be were normally copied from the originals kept in the monastery without interference of any kind from other MSS (subsection 4.3.3) and since nothing argues against this idea, Bea has been connected directly to 0. existence of the sixth branch (the PO branch) is doubtful.
